900 <section id="_1">

901

902 <h2>

903General Information About Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

904

905 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

906<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

907<h6>

908In This Section</h6>

909<ul>

910<li>

911<a href="#_254_toc">

912Incidence and Mortality</a>

913</li>

914<li>

915<a href="#_340_toc">

916Risk Factors</a>

917</li>

918<li>

919<a href="#_1394_toc">

920Clinical Features</a>

921</li>

922<li>

923<a href="#_344_toc">

924Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

925</li>

926<li>

927<a href="#_346_toc">

928Prognostic Factors</a>

929</li>

930<li>

931<a href="#_369_toc">

932Follow-up Testing</a>

933</li>

934</ul>

935</nav>

936<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

937<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/patient/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq">

938Go to Patient Version</a>

939</div>

940<section id="_254">

941<h3 id="_254_toc">

942Incidence and Mortality</h3>

943<p id="_255" tabindex="-1">

944Estimated new cases and deaths from soft tissue sarcoma in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9451</a>

946]</p>

947<div class="pdq-content-list">

948<ul id="_256">

949<li>

950New cases: 13,400.</li>

951<li>

952Deaths: 5,140.</li>

953</ul>

954</div>

955</section>

956<p id="_3" tabindex="-1">

957The reported international incidence of soft tissue sarcoma ranges from 1.8 to 5 cases per 100,000 individuals per year.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9582</a>

959] The rate of new cases of soft tissue cancer in the United States was 3.4 per 100,000 people per year. The death rate was 1.3 per 100,000 people per year. These rates are age-adjusted and based on cases from 2015 through 2019 and deaths from 2016 through 2020.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9603</a>

961]</p>

962<section id="_340">

963<h3 id="_340_toc">

964Risk Factors</h3>

965<p id="_356" tabindex="-1">

966Most soft tissue sarcomas are sporadic. Risk factors include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9674</a>

968-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9696</a>

970]</p>

971<div class="pdq-content-list">

972<ul id="_1390">

973<li>

974Previous radiation therapy.</li>

975<li>

976Chronic lymphedema (risk factor for lymphangiosarcoma).</li>

977<li>

978Exposure to the chemicals thorium dioxide (Thorotrast), vinyl chloride, or arsenic. These are established carcinogens for hepatic angiosarcomas.</li>

979<li>

980HIV and human herpesvirus 8 infection. These viruses have been implicated in the pathogenesis of Kaposi sarcoma. For more information, see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/kaposi-treatment-pdq">

981Kaposi Sarcoma Treatment</a>

982.</li>

983</ul>

984</div>

985<p id="_2102" tabindex="-1">

986Soft tissue sarcomas occur more frequently in patients with the following inherited syndromes:[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9874</a>

988-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9896</a>

990]</p>

991<div class="pdq-content-list">

992<ul id="_2103">

993<li>

994 Li-Fraumeni

995syndrome (<em class="gene-name">

996TP53</em>

997 mutation).</li>

998<li>

999von Recklinghausen disease (neurofibromatosis type 1; <em class="gene-name">

1000NF1</em>

1001 mutation).</li>

1002<li>

1003Gardner syndrome (<em class="gene-name">

1004APC</em>

1005 mutation).</li>

1006<li>

1007Nevoid basal cell carcinoma syndrome (Gorlin syndrome; <em class="gene-name">

1008PTCH1</em>

1009 mutation).</li>

1010<li>

1011Tuberous sclerosis (Bourneville disease; <em class="gene-name">

1012TSC1</em>

1013 or <em class="gene-name">

1014TSC2</em>

1015 mutation).</li>

1016<li>

1017Werner

1018syndrome (adult progeria; <em class="gene-name">

1019WRN</em>

1020 mutation).</li>

1021</ul>

1022</div>

1023</section>

1024<section id="_1394">

1025<h3 id="_1394_toc">

1026Clinical Features</h3>

1027<p id="_1395" tabindex="-1">

1028Soft tissue sarcomas are a heterogenous family of malignant tumors that may arise in nearly any organ system. The anatomical distribution in adults is as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10295</a>

1030] </p>

1031<div class="pdq-content-list">

1032<ul id="_2104">

1033<li>

1034Extremities (45%).</li>

1035<li>

1036Intra-abdominal organs (38%).</li>

1037<li>

1038Trunk (10%).</li>

1039<li>

1040Head and neck (5%).</li>

1041</ul>

1042</div>

1043</section>

1044<section id="_344">

1045<h3 id="_344_toc">

1046Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1047<p id="_345" tabindex="-1">

1048 Adequate

1049tissue should be obtained via either image-guided core-needle biopsy or planned incisional biopsy (for select cases). The samples should be reviewed by a pathologist who is experienced in diagnosing sarcomas. Careful

1050planning of the initial biopsy, with consultation among the surgeon, radiation oncologist, and interventional radiologist, is important to avoid compromising subsequent

1051curative resection. In general, incisional biopsies are reserved for patients whose prior core-needle biopsies were nondiagnostic or when a core-needle biopsy cannot be safely performed because of anatomical constraints. </p>

1052<p id="_1667" tabindex="-1">

1053Before any intervention is initiated, imaging is performed to evaluate the sarcoma and determine if there are metastases. The following modalities may be used as clinically indicated:</p>

1054<div class="pdq-content-list">

1055<ul id="_1983">

1056<li>

1057Plain radiography.</li>

1058<li>

1059Computed tomography (CT).</li>

1060<li>

1061Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).</li>

1062<li>

1063Positron emission tomography (PET) scan and bone scan. May be used along with CT. PET imaging is particularly useful for sarcoma subtypes with a propensity for lymph node metastases (e.g., synovial sarcoma, clear cell sarcoma, angiosarcoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, and epithelioid sarcoma). </li>

1064</ul>

1065</div>

1066</section>

1067<section id="_346">

1068<h3 id="_346_toc">

1069Prognostic Factors</h3>

1070<p id="_347" tabindex="-1">

1071Factors for a poor prognosis in adults with soft tissue sarcomas include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

10724</a>

1073-<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

107415</a>

1075]</p>

1076<div class="pdq-content-list">

1077<ul id="_348">

1078<li>

1079Age

1080older than 60 years.</li>

1081<li>

1082Tumor size (e.g., larger than 5 cm in greatest dimension, variable impact depending on sarcoma subtype).</li>

1083<li>

1084High histological grade of the tumor (incorporating histology-specific differentiation, mitotic rate, and extent of tumor necrosis).</li>

1085<li>

1086Advanced pathological stage of the tumor at the time of diagnosis.</li>

1087<li>

1088Positive tumor margins after surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

108910</a>

1090]</li>

1091</ul>

1092</div>

1093</section>

1094<p id="_110" tabindex="-1">

1095Small low-grade tumors, particularly in the trunk or extremities, are frequently curable by surgery alone. Higher-grade sarcomas are associated with higher local-treatment failure

1096rates and increased metastatic potential.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

109716</a>

1098]

1099</p>

1100<p id="_1338" tabindex="-1">

1101Prognostic nomograms (incorporating specific variables) have been developed for soft tissue sarcomas of the retroperitoneum and the extremities.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

110211</a>

1103-<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

110414</a>

1105]</p>

1106<section id="_369">

1107<h3 id="_369_toc">

1108Follow-up Testing</h3>

1109<p id="_1445" tabindex="-1">

1110PET and CT imaging may have higher sensitivity than contrast-enhanced CT imaging when recurrent sarcoma is suspected. Late recurrences (more than 5 years from initial diagnosis) are seen with some histologies, such as synovial sarcoma or alveolar soft-part sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

111117</a>

1112,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

111318</a>

1114] </p>

1115<p id="_1446" tabindex="-1">

1116Evidence (posttreatment imaging surveillance):</p>

1117<div class="pdq-content-list">

1118<ol id="_1447">

1119<li>

1120A retrospective review included 174 consecutive patients with soft tissue sarcoma of the limb who underwent follow-up by oncologists at a single center from 2003 to 2009.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

112119</a>

1122][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1123Level of evidence C2</a>

1124] The rate and site of recurrence and mode of detection were analyzed. The following results were reported: <div class="pdq-content-list">

1125<ol id="_2337" class="lower-alpha">

1126<li>

1127Eighty-two patients (47%) experienced relapse. </li>

1128<li>

1129Isolated local recurrences occurred in 26 patients, and local relapse with synchronous pulmonary metastases occurred in 5 patients.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1130<ul id="_1449">

1131<li>

1132Local recurrences were detected clinically in 30 of the 31 patients.</li>

1133<li>

1134MRI identified only one local recurrence.</li>

1135</ul>

1136</div>

1137</li>

1138<li>

1139Twenty-eight patients (16%) developed isolated lung metastases.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1140<ul id="_1450">

1141<li>

1142The lung metastases were amenable to resections in nine patients, seven of whom were free of disease after treatment. </li>

1143<li>

1144Lung metastases were detected by chest x-ray in 19 patients, by CT scan in 3 patients, and clinically in 11 patients.</li>

1145</ul>

1146</div>

1147</li>

1148<li>

1149Twenty-three patients developed nonpulmonary metastases. </li>

1150<li>

1151More than 80% of the relapses occurred in the first 2 years of follow-up. Later recurrences were also observed.</li>

1152</ol>

1153</div>

1154</li>

1155</ol>

1156</div>

1157<p id="_1451" tabindex="-1">

1158This study supports imaging surveillance for detection of lung metastases. Local recurrences at the primary site were usually detected by clinical examination. The impact of metastases detection on overall survival or quality of life is unknown.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

115919</a>

1160]</p>

1161</section>

1242 <section id="_8">

1243

1244 <h2>

1245Cellular Classification of Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

1246

1247 <div id="_section_8" class="pdq-sections">

1248<p id="_1396" tabindex="-1">

1249Soft tissue sarcomas are heterogeneous, with more than 100 different entities described in the World Health Organization (WHO) 2020 classification.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

12501</a>

1251]</p>

1252<p id="_263" tabindex="-1">

1253Soft tissue sarcomas are classified histologically according to the presumed tissue of origin. Immunohistochemistry,

1254flow cytometry, molecular profiling, and, rarely, electron microscopy may identify particular subtypes within the major histological categories. Some subtypes of sarcomas are characterized by genetic events such as chromosomal translocations (e.g., translocation t(X;18)(p11;q11) in synovial sarcomas and translocation t(12;16)(q13;p11) in myxoid liposarcomas).[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

12552</a>

1256-<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

12574</a>

1258]</p>

1259<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1260References</h6>

1261<ol>

1262<li id="section_2.1">

1263WHO Classification of Tumours Editorial Board, eds: Soft Tissue and Bone Tumours. IARC Press; 2020. WHO Classification of Tumours. 5th ed; Vol 3.</li>

1264<li id="section_2.2">

1265Singer S, Antonescu CR: Molecular biology of sarcomas. In: DeVita VT Jr, Lawrence TS, Rosenberg SA, et al., eds.: DeVita, Hellman, and Rosenberg&#8217;s Cancer: Principles &amp; Practice of Oncology. 11th ed. Wolters Kluwer, 2019, pp 1384-99.</li>

1266<li id="section_2.3">

1267Singer S, Tap WD, Kirsch DG: Soft tissue sarcoma. In: DeVita VT Jr, Lawrence TS, Rosenberg SA, et al., eds.: DeVita, Hellman, and Rosenberg&#8217;s Cancer: Principles &amp; Practice of Oncology. 11th ed. Wolters Kluwer, 2019, pp 1400-49.</li>

1268<li id="section_2.4">

1269O&#8217;Donnell RJ, DuBois SG, Haas-Kogan DA: Sarcomas of bone. In: DeVita VT Jr, Lawrence TS, Rosenberg SA, et al., eds.: DeVita, Hellman, and Rosenberg&#8217;s Cancer: Principles &amp; Practice of Oncology. 11th ed. Wolters Kluwer, 2019, pp 1450-74.</li>

1270</ol>

1279 <h2>

1280Stage Information for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

1281

1282 <div id="_section_193" class="pdq-sections">

1283<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1284<h6>

1285In This Section</h6>

1286<ul>

1287<li>

1288<a href="#_1397_toc">

1289Staging Evaluation</a>

1290</li>

1291<li>

1292<a href="#_2109_toc">

1293American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) Staging System<ul>

1294<li>

1295<a href="#_1407_toc">

1296TNM definitions</a>

1297</li>

1298<li>

1299<a href="#_1408_toc">

1300FNCLCC histological grade </a>

1301</li>

1302<li>

1303<a href="#_1410_toc">

1311<section id="_1397">

1312<h3 id="_1397_toc">

1313Staging Evaluation</h3>

1314<p id="_1398" tabindex="-1">

1315In addition to histology, identifying the location and extent of disease (e.g., localized, locally advanced, metastatic) is important in determining the most effective initial treatment for

1316patients with soft tissue sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

13171</a>

1318]</p>

1319<p id="_1399" tabindex="-1">

1320Imaging tests used in the staging evaluation may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

13211</a>

1322-<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

13235</a>

1324]</p>

1325<div class="pdq-content-list">

1326<ul id="_1400">

1327<li>

1328Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) and/or computed tomography (CT) scan of the primary tumor area. </li>

1329<li>

1330Chest CT scan for high-grade sarcomas to look for metastasis to the lung (the most common site of distant spread).</li>

1331<li>

1332 Abdominal/pelvic CT scan.</li>

1333<li>

1334 Positron emission tomography (PET) scan.</li>

1335</ul>

1336</div>

1337<p id="_2106" tabindex="-1">

1338 Some staging evaluation procedures depend on the tumor histology and site, including the following:</p>

1339<div class="pdq-content-list">

1340<ul id="_2107">

1341<li>

1342CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis and whole spine MRI for round cell and myxoid liposarcomas, as these histologies can have extrapulmonary spread to unusual sites.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

13436</a>

1344]</li>

1345<li>

1346Brain imaging for subtypes that have a higher propensity for central nervous system involvement, such as angiosarcoma, epithelioid sarcoma, or alveolar soft-part sarcoma.</li>

1347</ul>

1348</div>

1349<p id="_1401" tabindex="-1">

1350Knowledge of intracompartmental or extracompartmental extension of extremity sarcomas is

1351important for surgical decision making. For complete staging, a thorough

1352review of all

1353biopsy specimens, including those from the primary tumor, lymph nodes, or other

1354suspicious lesions, is essential. Nodal involvement is rare, occurring in

1355less than 3% of patients with sarcoma, but it occurs more often in certain subtypes, such as rhabdomyosarcoma, angiosarcoma, synovial sarcoma, clear cell sarcoma, and epithelioid sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

13567</a>

1357,<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

13588</a>

1359]</p>

1360</section>

1361<section id="_2109">

1362<h3 id="_2109_toc">

1363American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) Staging System</h3>

1364<p id="_2110" tabindex="-1">

1365The 2017 AJCC/Union for International Cancer Control (UICC) cancer staging classification system recommends the use of the three-tiered French Federation of Comprehensive Cancer Centers (F&#233;d&#233;ration Nationale des Centres de Lutte Contre Le Cancer [FNCLCC]) Sarcoma Group grading schema. Prognostic factors required for stage grouping are from FNCLCC. The definitions of grade are provided in <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1329">

1366Table 6</a>

1367. Of note, staging is primarily used as a research tool and does not routinely impact decision making outside of the factors listed above (sarcoma subtype and grade, primary location, extent of disease [localized, locally advanced, distant metastases]).</p>

1368<p id="_2111" tabindex="-1">

1369The AJCC staging system has designated stage by the following criteria:[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

13701</a>

1371-<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

13725</a>

1373]</p>

1374<div class="pdq-content-list">

1375<ul id="_2161">

1376<li>

1377Tumor size, nodal status, metastasis, histological grade (TNMG). </li>

1378<li>

1379 Anatomical primary tumor site (head and neck, trunk and extremities, abdomen and thoracic visceral organs, retroperitoneum, and unusual histologies and sites).</li>

1380</ul>

1381</div>

1382<p id="_2162" tabindex="-1">

1383For information on unusual histologies and sites, see the eighth edition of the <em>

1384AJCC Cancer Staging Manual</em>

1385,[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

13861</a>

1387] which indicates that the TNMG staging classification has different T staging criteria and prognostic groups based on the primary tumor site (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1314">

1388Table 1</a>

1389). The characteristic molecular markers of some sarcomas are not formally incorporated in the staging system pending further evaluation of their impact on prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

13902</a>

1391-<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

13925</a>

1393]</p>

1394<p id="_2171" tabindex="-1">

1395Recurrent sarcomas are restaged using the same system that is used for primary tumors, with the notation that the tumor is recurrent.</p>

1396<section id="_1407">

1676<section id="_1408">

1677<h4 id="_1408_toc">

1678FNCLCC histological grade </h4>

1679<p id="_701" tabindex="-1">

1680The histological grade of the sarcoma is an important factor in staging all soft tissue sarcomas. It is determined by the following three parameters: </p>

1681<div class="pdq-content-list">

1682<ul id="_2124">

1683<li>

1684Histology-specific tumor differentiation (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2169">

1685Table 4</a>

1686).</li>

1687<li>

1688Mitotic activity.</li>

1689<li>

1690 Extent of tumor necrosis.</li>

1691</ul>

1692</div>

1693<p id="_2125" tabindex="-1">

1694The purpose of the grading system is to predict which patients will develop metastasis and therefore benefit from postoperative chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

16959</a>

1696,<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

169710</a>

1698] Each parameter is scored, and the scores are then added to determine the FNCLCC histological grade (see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2053">

1699Table 5</a>

1700 and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1329">

1701Table 6</a>

1702).</p>

1703<table id="_2169" class="table-default expandable-container">

2216Pollock RE, Maki RG: Introduction to soft tissue sarcoma. In: Amin MB, Edge SB, Greene FL, et al., eds.: AJCC Cancer Staging Manual. 8th ed. Springer; 2017, pp 489-97.</li>

2217</ol>

2218</div>

2219

2220 </section>

2221

2304<section id="_2185">

2305<h3 id="_2185_toc">

2306Planning Therapy</h3>

2307<p id="_2186" tabindex="-1">

2308Complete

2309staging and treatment planning by a multidisciplinary team of cancer

2310specialists is required to determine the optimal treatment for patients with soft tissue sarcoma. In most cases, a combined modality approach of preoperative radiation therapy (preRT) or postoperative radiation therapy (PORT) is used for treatment, rather than a radical surgical procedure such as amputation.

2311Surgery without PORT may be possible in selected cases. The role of chemotherapy is not well defined.</p>

2312<section id="_2187">

2313<h4 id="_2187_toc">

2314Specialized centers</h4>

2315<p id="_2188" tabindex="-1">

2316There is evidence that favorable clinical outcomes may be associated with referral to a specialized sarcoma treatment center.</p>

2317<p id="_2189" tabindex="-1">

2318Evidence (referral to a specialized sarcoma treatment center):</p>

2319<div class="pdq-content-list">

2320<ol id="_2190">

2321<li>

2322An analysis of a population-based consecutive series of 375 patients with soft tissue sarcoma in Sweden reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

23231</a>

2324][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2325Level of evidence C2</a>

2326]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2327<ul id="_2191">

2328<li>

2329The local recurrence rate was 45% (35 of 78 patients) in patients who were not referred.</li>

2330<li>

2331The local recurrence rate was 24% (24 of 102 patients) in patients who were referred after initial surgery or incisional biopsy.</li>

2332<li>

2333The local recurrence rate was 18% (36 of 195 patients) in patients who were referred before any surgical procedure.</li>

2334<li>

2335Local recurrence rates in patients with resected tumors were higher in those who were not referred (for the primary tumor) to the specialized center (<em>

2336P</em>

2337 = .0001 for the difference between those not referred vs. those referred before any surgical procedure).</li>

2338<li>

2339There were no statistically significant differences in death from sarcoma among the groups. </li>

2340</ul>

2341</div>

2342</li>

2343<li>

2344A British study of 260 patients with soft tissue sarcoma diagnosed within a 3-year period reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

23452</a>

2346]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2347<ul id="_2192">

2348<li>

2349Thirty-seven percent of the patients had most of their treatment at a specialized center.</li>

2350<li>

2351The remaining 63% were treated at 38 different hospitals.</li>

2352<li>

2353The rate of local recurrence was 19% for patients treated at the specialized center.</li>

2354<li>

2355The rate of local recurrence was 39% for patients treated at the district general hospitals, even though the tumors in these patients were smaller and of lower grade.</li>

2356<li>

2357The most significant factors affecting survival were tumor grade (high grade vs. low grade) and the depth of the tumor.</li>

2358<li>

2359Patients treated at the specialized center had a small survival advantage after multivariate analysis.</li>

2360</ul>

2361</div>

2362</li>

2363</ol>

2364</div>

2365</section>

2366</section>

2497</section>

2498<section id="_278">

2499<h3 id="_278_toc">

2500Surgery</h3>

2501<p id="_2195" tabindex="-1">

2502Surgical resection is the mainstay of therapy for soft tissue sarcomas. </p>

2503<p id="_2196" tabindex="-1">

2504In some small low-grade tumors of the extremities or trunk, surgery alone can be performed without the use of radiation. Evidence for this approach is limited to single-institution, relatively small case series [<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

25053</a>

2506-<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

25075</a>

2508] or analysis of outcomes in the National Cancer Institute's Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program tumor registry.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

25096</a>

2510] These comparisons suffer from low statistical power and differential evaluability rates that could have introduced bias.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

25113</a>

2512]</p>

2513<p id="_2197" tabindex="-1">

2514Patient selection factors may vary among surgeons. In general, surgery alone is considered in patients with low-grade tumors of the extremity or superficial trunk that are 5 cm or smaller in diameter (T1) and have microscopically negative surgical margins. In these patients, long-term local tumor control is about 90%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

25157</a>

2516]</p>

2517<section id="_2198">

2518<h4 id="_2198_toc">

2519Extremity tumors</h4>

2520<p id="_2199" tabindex="-1">

2521When feasible, wide-margin function-sparing surgical excision is the cornerstone of effective treatment for extremity tumors. This may be facilitated by soft tissue reconstructive surgery, which generally permits wider margins than those obtained when the surgical plan involves direct closure of the excision site.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

25228</a>

2523] Cutting into the tumor mass or shelling out the gross tumor is associated with an elevated risk of local recurrence. Even for high-grade disease, soft tissue sarcomas of the extremities can usually be effectively treated while preserving the limb with combined-modality treatment consisting of preRT or PORT to reduce local recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

25249</a>

2525] For more information, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_286">

2526Radiation Therapy</a>

2527 section.</p>

2528<p id="_2200" tabindex="-1">

2529Evidence (amputation vs. limb-sparing surgery):</p>

2530<div class="pdq-content-list">

2531<ol id="_2201">

2532<li>

2533A retrospective study analyzed a prospective sarcoma database of 649 patients with extremity soft tissue sarcoma. Ninety-two patients underwent amputation, and 557 patients had a limb-sparing procedure. Patients who underwent amputation had large (&#8805;5 cm) high-grade tumors that invaded major vascular or nervous structures.[<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

253410</a>

2535]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2536<ul id="_2334">

2537<li>

2538The patients who underwent amputation achieved significantly better local

2539control than the patients who had a limb-sparing procedure (<em>

2540P</em>

2541 = .007). </li>

2542<li>

2543However, no survival

2544benefit was demonstrated in the patients selected

2545for amputation (i.e., patients with large high-grade tumors) when compared

2546with patients undergoing a limb-sparing procedure with similar

2547tumors.</li>

2548<li>

2549Prevention of local recurrence by amputation did

2550not improve survival in this group compared with similar patients

2551undergoing limb-sparing surgery who did develop a local recurrence.</li>

2552</ul>

2553</div>

2554</li>

2555<li>

2556A retrospective study included 769 patients with a high-grade sarcoma of the extremities who underwent a limb-sparing surgery. Eighty-nine patients were treated with neoadjuvant radiation therapy, 315 patients were treated with adjuvant irradiation, and 365 patients were treated with surgery alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

255711</a>

2558] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2559<ul id="_2335">

2560<li>

2561After a mean follow-up of 45 months, 95 local recurrences occurred, resulting in a local recurrence-free survival rate of 83.2% after 5 years and 75.9% after 10 years.</li>

2562<li>

2563 Neoadjuvant radiation therapy provided the best local recurrence-free rate for 5 years (90.0%), but after 10 years (78.3%) adjuvant irradiation resulted in better local control.</li>

2564<li>

2565 Patients treated with neoadjuvant radiation therapy had the highest rate of revision surgery (9.0%), followed by patients who were treated with surgery alone (5.5%) and patients who received adjuvant irradiation (4.4%) (<em>

2566P</em>

2567 = .085). </li>

2568</ul>

2569</div>

2570</li>

2571</ol>

2572</div>

2573</section>

2574<section id="_2203">

2575<h4 id="_2203_toc">

2576Trunk and head/neck tumors</h4>

2577<p id="_2204" tabindex="-1">

2578Local control of high-grade soft tissue sarcomas of the trunk and the head and neck can be achieved with surgery in combination with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

257912</a>

2580] Surgery without PORT may be possible in selected patients. A case series was reported from a specialized sarcoma treatment referral center, in which 74 selected patients with primary extremity and trunk tumors 5 cm or smaller were found to have no histological involvement of the surgical margins.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

25813</a>

2582] These patients were observed without radiation therapy, and the estimated local recurrence rate after 10 years was 11%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

25833</a>

2584][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2585Level of evidence C3</a>

2586] </p>

2587</section>

2588<section id="_2205">

2589<h4 id="_2205_toc">

2590Retroperitoneal tumors</h4>

2591<p id="_2206" tabindex="-1">

2592Effective treatment of retroperitoneal sarcomas requires removal of all gross disease while sparing adjacent viscera not invaded by tumor. The prognosis for patients with high-grade retroperitoneal sarcomas is less favorable than for patients with tumors at other sites, partly because of the difficulty in completely resecting these tumors and the dose-limiting toxicity of high-dose radiation therapy on visceral organs.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

259313</a>

2594-<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

259516</a>

2596]</p>

2597<p id="_2207" tabindex="-1">

2598Local disease control is crucial in patients with retroperitoneal sarcomas. Disease-specific mortality caused by local recurrence (without synchronous metastasis) was reported in up to 77% of patients with retroperitoneal sarcomas compared with 9% of patients with extremity or trunk sarcomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

259917</a>

2600] An additional consideration in retroperitoneal sarcomas is the extent of surgery. </p>

2601<p id="_2209" tabindex="-1">

2602Evidence (extended surgical resection):</p>

2603<div class="pdq-content-list">

2604<ol id="_2210">

2605<li>

2606A series of 382 patients with retroperitoneal sarcoma in a multivariate analysis showed the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

260718</a>

2608]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2609<ul id="_2211">

2610<li>

2611Patients treated with extended surgical resection had a 3.3-fold lower rate of local recurrence compared with patients who underwent simple complete resection.</li>

2612<li>

2613Extended surgical resection was not associated with improved survival.</li>

2614<li>

2615In a follow-up analysis, a 66% overall survival (OS) rate was observed in the extended surgical resection cohort compared with a 48% OS rate in historic controls.[<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

261619</a>

2617] </li>

2618</ul>

2619</div>

2620</li>

2621</ol>

2622</div>

2623<p id="_2212" tabindex="-1">

2624An extended surgical approach has to be weighed against an increase in morbidity (resulting from surgical complications) and mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

262520</a>

2626-<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

262723</a>

2628]</p>

2629</section>

2630<section id="_2213">

2631<h4 id="_2213_toc">

2632Metastatic disease</h4>

2633<p id="_2214" tabindex="-1">

2634In the setting of distant metastasis, surgery may be associated with long-term disease-free survival (DFS) in patients with pulmonary metastasis and optimal underlying disease biology. This includes patients with a limited number of metastases and slow nodule growth who have undergone or are undergoing complete resection of the primary tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

263524</a>

2636-<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

263726</a>

2638] It is not clear to what degree the favorable outcomes are attributable to the efficacy of surgery or the careful selection of patients based on factors that are associated with less-aggressive disease.</p>

2639</section>

2640</section>

2641<section id="_286">

2642<h3 id="_286_toc">

2643Radiation Therapy</h3>

2644<p id="_2216" tabindex="-1">

2645A patterns-of-care study using SEER data was queried to identify patients undergoing surgery for trunk and extremity soft tissue sarcomas from 2004 to 2009.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

264627</a>

2647] Of 5,075 patients, 50% received radiation therapy. Radiation therapy was not given as recommended in a significant portion of patients undergoing treatment for soft tissue sarcoma in the United States. Although routine radiation therapy is not recommended for patients with stage I disease, 25% of them still underwent radiation. Even though routine radiation therapy is recommended for patients with stage II and III tumors, only 60% of them underwent radiation. Predictors of radiation therapy efficacy from the multivariate analysis included the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

264827</a>

2649][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2650Level of evidence C2</a>

2651]</p>

2652<div class="pdq-content-list">

2653<ul id="_2217">

2654<li>

2655Age younger than 50 years (odds ratio [OR], 1.57; 95% confidence interval [CI], 1.28&#8211;1.91).</li>

2656<li>

2657Malignant fibrous histiocytoma histology (OR, 1.47; 95% CI, 1.3&#8211;1.92).</li>

2658<li>

2659T2 disease (OR, 1.88; 95% CI, 1.60&#8211;2.20).</li>

2660<li>

2661High tumor grade (G3) (OR, 6.27; 95% CI, 5.10&#8211;7.72). </li>

2662</ul>

2663</div>

2664<p id="_2218" tabindex="-1">

2665Patients with stage III soft tissue sarcoma who received radiation therapy showed improved disease-specific survival at 5 years, compared with those who did not receive radiation therapy (68% vs. 46%, <em>

2666P</em>

2667&#8201;&lt; .001).</p>

2668<p id="_2219" tabindex="-1">

2669On occasion, surgical excision cannot be performed in the initial management of soft tissue sarcomas because the morbidity would be unacceptable or nearby critical organs make complete resection impossible. In such circumstances, radiation has been used as the primary therapy;[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

267028</a>

2671] however, this must be considered a treatment of last resort. Experience of radiation as the primary therapy is limited to retrospective case series from single centers.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

267228</a>

2673][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2674Level of evidence C3</a>

2675]</p>

2676<section id="_2220">

2677<h4 id="_2220_toc">

2678Extremity and trunk tumors </h4>

2679<p id="_2221" tabindex="-1">

2680Radiation plays an important role in limb-sparing therapy. Pre- and postoperative radiation therapy has been shown to decrease the risk of local recurrence. These techniques have not demonstrated increased OS in prospective trials, but they are used to avoid amputation for all but the most locally advanced tumors or in limbs seriously compromised by vascular disease, where acceptable functional preservation is not possible. In the case of external-bean radiation therapy (EBRT), irradiation of the entire limb circumference is avoided to preserve vascular and nerve structures that are critical to retain the function of the limb.</p>

2681<p id="_2222" tabindex="-1">

2682Evidence (PORT):</p>

2683<div class="pdq-content-list">

2684<ol id="_2223">

2685<li>

2686PORT has been tested in a single-institution randomized trial of 141 patients with extremity sarcomas who were treated with limb-sparing surgery. Patients with high-grade tumors (n = 91) also received adjuvant chemotherapy (five 28-day cycles of doxorubicin and cyclophosphamide). Patients were randomly assigned to receive either radiation therapy (45 Gy to a wide field, plus a tumor-bed boost of 18 Gy over 6&#8211;7 weeks) concurrently with chemotherapy (in the case of high-grade tumors) or no radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

268729</a>

2688][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2689Level of evidence B1</a>

2690] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2691<ul id="_2224">

2692<li>

2693At up to 12 years of follow-up, there was one local recurrence in the 70 patients randomly assigned to receive radiation therapy versus 17 recurrences in the 71 control patients (<em>

2694P</em>

2695 = .0001). There was a similar reduction in risk of local recurrence for both high- and low-grade tumors. </li>

2696<li>

2697There was no difference in OS between the radiation therapy group and the control group.</li>

2698<li>

2699Global quality of life was similar in the two groups, but the radiation therapy group had substantially worse functional deficits resulting from reduced strength, reduced joint motion, and increased edema.</li>

2700</ul>

2701</div>

2702</li>

2703</ol>

2704</div>

2705<p id="_2225" tabindex="-1">

2706To limit acute toxicity, smaller fields and lower doses of radiation are generally given with preRT than with PORT. PreRT has been directly compared with PORT for extremity soft tissue sarcomas in a multicenter randomized trial, and no significant difference in local control or OS rates was found.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

270730</a>

2708-<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

270932</a>

2710]</p>

2711<p id="_2226" tabindex="-1">

2712Evidence (preRT vs. PORT):</p>

2713<div class="pdq-content-list">

2714<ol id="_2227">

2715<li>

2716Though designed to include 266 patients, a trial was stopped after 190 patients were accrued because wound complications in the preRT group had increased. In the first phase of the trial, patients in the preRT group received wide-field radiation therapy of 50 Gy (in 2-Gy fractions). In the second phase of the trial, patients received an additional 16 Gy to 20 Gy to the tumor bed and a 2-cm margin (only if tumor cells were found at the surgical margins). Patients in the PORT group received radiation therapy during both phases of the trial.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

271730</a>

2718-<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

271932</a>

2720]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2721<ul id="_2229">

2722<li>

2723The wound complication rates were 35% in the preRT group and 17% in the PORT group (<em>

2724P</em>

2725 = .01). In addition, limb function at 6 weeks after surgery was worse in the preRT group (<em>

2726P</em>

2727 = .01).[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

272830</a>

2729] </li>

2730<li>

2731At 5 years, the two groups had similar local control rates (93% for the preRT group vs. 92% for the PORT group) and OS rates (73% for the preRT group vs. 67% for the PORT group; <em>

2732P</em>

2733 = .48).[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

273431</a>

2735] </li>

2736<li>

2737Of the 129 patients evaluated for limb function at 21 to 27 months after surgery (n = 73 for preRT and n = 56 for PORT), limb function was similar in both groups, but there was a statistical trend for less fibrosis in the preRT group (<em>

2738P</em>

2739 = .07).[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

274032</a>

2741]</li>

2742</ul>

2743</div>

2744</li>

2745</ol>

2746</div>

2747<p id="_2230" tabindex="-1">

2748Brachytherapy has also been investigated as an adjuvant therapy for soft tissue sarcomas. Although brachytherapy has the possible advantages of convenience and giving less radiation to normal surrounding tissue relative to EBRT, the two treatment strategies have not been directly compared in terms of efficacy or morbidity. However, adjuvant brachytherapy has been compared with surgery without radiation. </p>

2749<p id="_2231" tabindex="-1">

2750Evidence (surgery followed by brachytherapy vs. surgery alone):</p>

2751<div class="pdq-content-list">

2752<ol id="_2232">

2753<li>

2754In a single-institution trial, 164 patients with sarcomas of the extremity or superficial trunk were randomly assigned during surgery (if all gross tumor could be excised) to receive an iridium Ir 192 implant delivering 42 Gy to 45 Gy over 4 to 6 days (78 patients) or to a control arm of no radiation (86 patients).[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

275533</a>

2756,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

275734</a>

2758] Thirty-four patients in each study arm with high-grade tumors who were believed to be at risk for metastasis received adjuvant doxorubicin-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

275933</a>

2760][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2761Level of evidence B1</a>

2762] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2763<ul id="_2233">

2764<li>

2765With a median follow-up of 76 months, the 5-year actuarial local recurrence rates were 18% in the brachytherapy arm and 31% in the control arm (<em>

2766P</em>

2767 = .04). This difference was limited to patients with high-grade tumors. </li>

2768<li>

2769There was no discernible difference in sarcoma-specific survival rates between the brachytherapy arm (84%) and control arm (81%) (<em>

2770P</em>

2771 = .65), and there was no difference in patients with high-grade tumors.</li>

2772<li>

2773The rates of clinically important wound complications (e.g., the need for operative revision or repeated seroma drainage, wound separation, large hematomas, or purulent infection) were 24% in the brachytherapy arm and 14% in the control arm (<em>

2774P</em>

2775 = .13). The wound reoperation rates were 10% in the brachytherapy arm and 0% in the control arm (<em>

2776P</em>

2777 = .006).[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

277834</a>

2779]</li>

2780</ul>

2781</div>

2782</li>

2783</ol>

2784</div>

2785<p id="_2234" tabindex="-1">

2786Intensity-modulated radiation therapy (IMRT) has been used to deliver preRT or PORT to patients with extremity soft tissue sarcomas to spare the femur, joints, and selected other normal tissues from the full prescription dose, and thus maintain local control while potentially reducing radiation therapy&#8211;related morbidity. Initial single-institution reports suggest that high rates of local control with some reduction in morbidity are possible with IMRT.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

278735</a>

2788,<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

278936</a>

2790]</p>

2791<p id="_2235" tabindex="-1">

2792Retrospective comparison of IMRT and 3-dimensional, conformal radiation therapy demonstrated that local recurrence for primary soft tissue sarcomas of the extremity was worse in the non-IMRT group.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

279337</a>

2794][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2795Level of evidence C3</a>

2796] </p>

2797</section>

2798<section id="_2236">

2799<h4 id="_2236_toc">

2800Retroperitoneal tumors</h4>

2801<p id="_2237" tabindex="-1">

2802Retrospective data support the use of preRT or PORT versus surgery alone to treat retroperitoneal sarcomas.</p>

2803<p id="_2238" tabindex="-1">

2804Evidence (preRT or PORT vs. surgery alone):</p>

2805<div class="pdq-content-list">

2806<ol id="_2239">

2807<li>

2808Outcomes from a total of 9,068 patients in two case-control studies conducted between 2003 and 2011 were analyzed.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

280938</a>

2810]<p id="_2240" tabindex="-1">

2811The 2,196 patients who received PORT were compared with 2,196 matched controls.</p>

2812<div class="pdq-content-list">

2813<ul id="_2241">

2814<li>

2815The median OS was 89 months for the patients in the PORT group versus 64 months for the patients who did not receive radiation therapy. </li>

2816<li>

2817The 5-year survival rate was 60% for the patients in the PORT group and 52% for the patients who did not receive radiation therapy. </li>

2818</ul>

2819</div>

2820<p id="_2242" tabindex="-1">

2821The 563 patients who received preRT were compared with 1,126 matched controls.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

282238</a>

2823]</p>

2824<div class="pdq-content-list">

2825<ul id="_2243">

2826<li>

2827The median OS was 110 months for the patients who received preRT versus 66 months for the patients who did not receive radiation therapy. </li>

2828<li>

2829The 5-year survival rate was 62% for the patients who received preRT versus 54% for the patients who did not receive radiation therapy.</li>

2830</ul>

2831</div>

2832</li>

2833<li>

2834Two small prospective studies explored the role of neoadjuvant radiation therapy in patients with intermediate- or high-grade retroperitoneal sarcomas. In a combined analysis of the 54 patients who underwent R0 resection (resection for cure or complete remission) or R1 resection (resection to microscopic residual tumor) after preRT, the following results were reported:[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

283539</a>

2836]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2837<ul id="_2244">

2838<li>

2839The 5-year local relapse-free survival (RFS) rate was 60%.</li>

2840<li>

2841The 5-year DFS rate was 60%.</li>

2842<li>

2843The 5-year OS rate was 61%. The median OS had not been reached (&gt;60 months) at the time of the report.</li>

2844</ul>

2845</div>

2846</li>

2847</ol>

2848</div>

2849</section>

2850</section>

2851<section id="_293">

2852<h3 id="_293_toc">

2853Chemotherapy</h3>

2854<section id="_2246">

2855<h4 id="_2246_toc">

2856Adjuvant chemotherapy for clinically localized tumors</h4>

2857<p id="_2247" tabindex="-1">

2858The role of adjuvant chemotherapy remains controversial. Any potential benefits should be considered in the context of the short- and long-term toxicities of the chemotherapy regimen. </p>

2859<p id="_2248" tabindex="-1">

2860Several prospective, randomized trials were unable to determine conclusively whether doxorubicin-based adjuvant chemotherapy benefits adults with resectable soft tissue sarcomas. Most of these studies accrued small numbers of patients and did not demonstrate a metastasis-free survival or an OS benefit from adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

286112</a>

2862] There was wide interstudy variability among the reported trials, including differences in therapeutic regimens, drug doses, sample sizes, tumor sites, and tumor histological grades.</p>

2863<p id="_2249" tabindex="-1">

2864Evidence (doxorubicin-based adjuvant chemotherapy):</p>

2865<div class="pdq-content-list">

2866<ol id="_2250">

2867<li>

2868A quantitative meta-analysis of updated data from 1,568 individual patients in 14 trials of doxorubicin-based adjuvant chemotherapy was reported. Only a small proportion of patients in this meta-analysis were treated with ifosfamide, an agent with demonstrated activity against soft tissue sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

286940</a>

2870,<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

287141</a>

2872][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2873Level of evidence B1</a>

2874] The following results were reported:<div class="pdq-content-list">

2875<ul id="_2251">

2876<li>

2877An absolute benefit from adjuvant chemotherapy of 6% for local relapse-free interval (95% CI, 1%&#8211;10%), 10% for distant relapse-free interval (95% CI, 5%&#8211;15%), and 10% for RFS (95% CI, 5%&#8211;15%). </li>

2878<li>

2879No statistically significant OS benefit at 10 years was detected; the absolute difference was 4% (95% CI, -1% to +9%).</li>

2880<li>

2881A subset analysis suggested that patients with sarcomas of the extremities may have benefited from adjuvant chemotherapy, with a reported 7% absolute OS improvement at 10 years (hazard ratio [HR]<sub>

2882death</sub>

2883, 0.8; <em>

2884P</em>

2885 = .029).[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

288641</a>

2887]</li>

2888</ul>

2889</div>

2890</li>

2891</ol>

2892</div>

2893<p id="_2252" tabindex="-1">

2894Subsequent chemotherapy trials were performed using anthracycline and ifosfamide combinations in patients who primarily had extremity or trunk soft tissue sarcomas. The data are conflicting. </p>

2895<p id="_2253" tabindex="-1">

2896Evidence (anthracycline and ifosfamide or cyclophosphamide combinations):</p>

2897<div class="pdq-content-list">

2898<ol id="_2254">

2899<li>

2900In a small feasibility study, 59 patients with high-risk soft tissue sarcomas (58 of whom had an extremity or the trunk as the primary site) underwent primary resection plus PORT. They were then randomly assigned to observation versus a dose-dense regimen of six 14-day cycles of ifosfamide, dacarbazine (DTIC), and doxorubicin (IFADIC regimen) with granulocyte colony-stimulating factor (G-CSF) bone marrow support.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

290142</a>

2902]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2903<ul id="_2255">

2904<li>

2905There were no statistically significant differences in OS or RFS; however, the study was severely underpowered. </li>

2906</ul>

2907</div>

2908</li>

2909<li>

2910In a second trial performed by the Italian National Council for Research, high-risk patients were treated with local therapy (wide resection plus preRT or PORT, or amputation as clinically necessary) and were then randomly assigned to observation versus five 21-day cycles of 4-epidoxorubicin (epirubicin) plus ifosfamide (with mesna and G-CSF).[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

291143</a>

2912,<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

291344</a>

2914]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2915<ul id="_2256">

2916<li>

2917Based on power calculations, the planned study size was 190 patients, but the trial was stopped after 104 patients had been entered because an interim analysis revealed a statistically significant (<em>

2918P</em>

2919 = .001) difference in DFS favoring the chemotherapy arm. By the time of the initial peer-reviewed report of the study, the DFS still favored the chemotherapy arm (median DFS of 48 months vs. 16 months), but the <em>

2920P</em>

2921 value had risen to .04.[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

292243</a>

2923]</li>

2924<li>

2925Although there was no difference in metastasis-free survival at the time of the report, there was an improvement in median OS (75 months for the chemotherapy arm vs. 46 months for the observation arm; <em>

2926P</em>

2927 = .03). </li>

2928<li>

2929At the follow-up report (median follow-up of 89.6 months in a range of 56&#8211;119 months), OS differences were no longer statistically significant (58.5% in the chemotherapy arm vs. 43.1% in the observation arm; <em>

2930P</em>

2931 = .07).[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

293244</a>

2933] </li>

2934<li>

2935The DFS difference had also lost statistical significance (47.2% in the chemotherapy arm vs. 16.0% in the observation arm; <em>

2936P</em>

2937 = .09).[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

293844</a>

2939] </li>

2940</ul>

2941</div>

2942<p id="_2257" tabindex="-1">

2943In summary, the trial was underpowered because it was stopped early, and the promising early results that led to stopping the trial diminished as the trial matured.</p>

2944</li>

2945<li>

2946In a third, underpowered, single-center trial, 88 patients with high-risk soft tissue sarcomas (64 with extremity or trunk primary tumors) underwent surgery (with or without radiation) and were then randomly assigned to receive four 21-day cycles of chemotherapy, epirubicin (n = 26) or epirubicin plus ifosfamide (n = 19), versus no adjuvant chemotherapy (n = 43).[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

294745</a>

2948] The trial was closed prematurely because of a slow accrual rate. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2949<ul id="_2258">

2950<li>

2951After a median follow-up of 94 months, the 5-year DFS rates were 69% in the chemotherapy arm and 44% in the control arm (<em>

2952P</em>

2953 = .01).</li>

2954<li>

2955The 5-year OS rates were 72% in the chemotherapy arm and 47% in the control arm (<em>

2956P</em>

2957 = .06).</li>

2958<li>

2959All the benefit associated with chemotherapy appeared restricted to the 19 patients who received epirubicin plus ifosfamide.</li>

2960</ul>

2961</div>

2962</li>

2963<li>

2964In another underpowered trial, 134 patients with high-risk soft tissue sarcomas (93% with extremity or trunk primary tumors) were randomly assigned to undergo surgical resection (with or without radiation) or to receive three preoperative 21-day cycles of doxorubicin plus ifosfamide.[<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

296546</a>

2966] This multicenter trial from the European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer (EORTC) (<a href="http://www.eortc.org/research_field/clinical-detail/62874/" title="http://www.eortc.org/research_field/clinical-detail/62874/">

2967EORTC-62874</a>

2968) was closed because of slow accrual and results that were not promising enough to warrant continuation of the trial. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2969<ul id="_2259">

2970<li>

2971With a median follow-up of 7.3 years, the 5-year DFS rates were 52% in the surgery-alone arm and 56% in the chemotherapy-plus-surgery arm (<em>

2972P</em>

2973 = .35).</li>

2974<li>

2975The 5-year OS rates were 64% in the surgery-alone arm and 65% in the chemotherapy-plus-surgery arm (<em>

2976P</em>

2977 = .22). </li>

2978</ul>

2979</div>

2980</li>

2981<li>

2982The previous four trials have been combined with the 14 first-generation trials in a trial-level meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

298347</a>

2984] Of the 18 randomized trials of patients with resectable soft tissue sarcomas, 5 trials used a combination of doxorubicin (50&#8211;90 mg/m<span class="sup">

29852</span>

2986 per cycle) plus ifosfamide (1,500&#8211;5,000 mg/m<span class="sup">

29872</span>

2988 per cycle). The remaining 13 trials used doxorubicin (50&#8211;70 mg/m<span class="sup">

29892</span>

2990 per cycle) alone or with other drugs.[<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

299147</a>

2992][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2993Level of evidence A1</a>

2994]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2995<ul id="_2260">

2996<li>

2997The absolute risk reduction in local recurrence rates associated with any chemotherapy added to local therapy was 4% (95% CI, 0%&#8211;7%), and it was 5% (95% CI, 1%&#8211;12%) when ifosfamide was combined with doxorubicin. </li>

2998<li>

2999The absolute reduction in overall mortality was 6% with any chemotherapy (95% CI, 2%&#8211;11%; this was a reduction from 46% to 40%), 11% for doxorubicin plus ifosfamide (95% CI, 3%&#8211;19%; this was a reduction from 41% to 30%), and 5% for doxorubicin without ifosfamide.</li>

3000</ul>

3001</div>

3002</li>

3003<li>

3004An additional multicenter randomized trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002641">

3005EORTC-62931</a>

3006 [NCT00002641]) that used adjuvant doxorubicin (75 mg/m<span class="sup">

30072</span>

3008) plus ifosfamide (5,000 mg/m<span class="sup">

30092</span>

3010) was not included in the previous meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.48">

301148</a>

3012] After local therapy, 351 patients were randomly assigned to five 21-day cycles of adjuvant therapy versus observation. The results of <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002641">

3013EORTC-62931</a>

3014 differed from those reported in the previous meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

301547</a>

3016] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3017<ul id="_2261">

3018<li>

3019The OS rate did not differ significantly between groups (HR, 0.94; 95% CI, 0.68&#8211;1.31; <em>

3020P</em>

3021 = .72); neither did the RFS rate (HR, 0.91, 0.67&#8211;1.22; <em>

3022P</em>

3023 = .51). </li>

3024<li>

3025The 5-year OS rates were 66.5% (95% CI, 58.8%&#8211;73%) in the chemotherapy group and 67.8% (95% CI, 60.3%&#8211;74.2%) in the control group. </li>

3026</ul>

3027</div>

3028</li>

3029<li>

3030In a subsequent analysis of pooled individual patient data, the EORTC investigators combined data from this trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002641">

3031EORTC-62931</a>

3032) with data from their previous trial (<a href="http://www.eortc.org/research_field/clinical-detail/62771/" title="http://www.eortc.org/research_field/clinical-detail/62771/">

3033EORTC-62771</a>

3034) [<a href="#cit/section_4.49">

303549</a>

3036] of adjuvant cyclophosphamide plus doxorubicin plus dacarbazine (CYVADIC), representing the two largest trials of adjuvant therapy for adults with soft tissue sarcoma in the literature (N = 819 patients).[<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

303750</a>

3038] [<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3039Level of evidence A1</a>

3040];[<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

304151</a>

3042]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3043<ul id="_2262">

3044<li>

3045Large tumor size, high histological grade, and R1 resection emerged as independent adverse prognostic factors for RFS and OS.</li>

3046<li>

3047Adjuvant chemotherapy was an independent favorable prognostic factor for RFS but not for OS.</li>

3048<li>

3049Males and patients older than 40 years had a significantly better RFS in the treatment arms, while adjuvant chemotherapy was associated with a marginally worse OS in females and patients younger than 40 years.</li>

3050<li>

3051The combined analysis showed no improvement in either RFS or OS associated with adjuvant chemotherapy.</li>

3052</ul>

3053</div>

3054</li>

3055</ol>

3056</div>

3057<p id="_2263" tabindex="-1">

3058In summary, the impact of adjuvant chemotherapy on survival is still controversial but is likely to be small in absolute magnitude. </p>

3059</section>

3060<section id="_2264">

3061<h4 id="_2264_toc">

3062Neoadjuvant chemotherapy</h4>

3063<p id="_2265" tabindex="-1">

3064In prospective studies, neoadjuvant chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy has shown response rates of 17% to 32%, 10-year RFS rates of up to 58%, and 10-year OS rates of up to 64%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

306546</a>

3066,<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

306752</a>

3068-<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

306956</a>

3070]</p>

3071<p id="_2266" tabindex="-1">

3072In retrospective studies, neoadjuvant chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy has resulted in DFS rates of 80% to 90% compared with about 60% in historical controls.[<a href="#cit/section_4.57">

307357</a>

3074-<a href="#cit/section_4.59">

307559</a>

3076]</p>

3077<p id="_2267" tabindex="-1">

3078A combined analysis of the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002791">

3079RTOG-9514</a>

3080 study (NCT00002791) of neoadjuvant chemoradiation and the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00589121">

3081RTOG-0630</a>

3082 study (NCT00589121) of neoadjuvant radiation therapy showed rates of pathological complete response of 27.5% in patients on neoadjuvant chemoradiation and 19.4% in patients on neoadjuvant radiation therapy in 123 evaluable patients. At a median follow-up of more than 5 years, the OS rate was 100% in patients with pathological complete responses; 5-year survival rates were 76.5% (RTOG-9514) and 56.4% (RTOG-0630) for patients who did not achieve pathological complete responses.[<a href="#cit/section_4.60">

308360</a>

3084]</p>

3085<p id="_2268" tabindex="-1">

3086Evidence (neoadjuvant histotype-tailored chemotherapy vs. standard chemotherapy):</p>

3087<div class="pdq-content-list">

3088<ol id="_2269">

3089<li>

3090The phase III <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01710176">

3091ISG-STS1001</a>

3092 study (NCT01710176) enrolled 164 patients with high-risk features (grade 3 tumor or grade 2 tumor with more than 50% necrosis, size &#8805;5 cm, deep location).[<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

309356</a>

3094] Patients were randomly assigned to receive either standard chemotherapy or histotype-tailored chemotherapy for three cycles as listed below:<div class="pdq-content-list">

3095<ol id="_2270" class="lower-alpha">

3096<li>

3097Standard chemotherapy (every 21 days): <div class="pdq-content-list">

3098<ul id="_2271">

3099<li>

3100Epirubicin, 60 mg/m<span class="sup">

31012</span>

3102, on days 1 and 2 plus ifosfamide, 3 g/m<span class="sup">

31032</span>

3104, on days 1, 2, and 3.</li>

3105</ul>

3106</div>

3107</li>

3108<li>

3109Histotype-tailored chemotherapy:<div class="pdq-content-list">

3110<ul id="_2272">

3111<li>

3112Undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma: docetaxel, 75 mg/m<span class="sup">

31132</span>

3114, on day 8 plus gemcitabine, 900 mg/m<span class="sup">

31152</span>

3116, on days 1 and 8 (every 21 days).</li>

3117<li>

3118Myxoid liposarcoma: trabectedin, 1.3 mg/m<span class="sup">

31192</span>

3120, continuous infusion (every 21 days).</li>

3121<li>

3122Synovial sarcoma: ifosfamide, 14 g/m<span class="sup">

31232</span>

3124, 14 days of continuous infusion (every 28 days).</li>

3125<li>

3126Malignant peripheral nerve sheath tumor: ifosfamide, 3 g/m<span class="sup">

31272</span>

3128, on days 1, 2, and 3 plus etoposide, 150 mg/m<span class="sup">

31292</span>

3130, on days 1, 2, and 3 (every 21 days).</li>

3131<li>

3132Leiomyosarcoma: gemcitabine, 1,800 mg/m<span class="sup">

31332</span>

3134, on day 1 plus dacarbazine, 500 mg/m<span class="sup">

31352</span>

3136, on day 1 (every 14 days).</li>

3137</ul>

3138</div>

3139</li>

3140</ol>

3141</div>

3142<p id="_2273" tabindex="-1">

3143The following results were reported:</p>

3144<div class="pdq-content-list">

3145<ul id="_2274">

3146<li>

3147With a median follow-up of 12.3 months, the projected RFS rate at 46 months was 62% (95% CI, 48%&#8211;77%) for patients who received standard chemotherapy versus 38% (95% CI, 22%&#8211;<em>

3148P</em>

3149 = .004). </li>

3150<li>

3151The OS rate was 89% (95% CI, 78%&#8211;99%) in the standard chemotherapy group versus 64% (95% CI, 27%&#8211;100%) in the histotype-tailored chemotherapy group (<em>

3152P</em>

3153 = .034). </li>

3154<li>

3155Trabectedin for myxoid liposarcoma had outcomes similar to those for the standard chemotherapy group.[<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

315656</a>

3157]</li>

3158</ul>

3159</div>

3160</li>

3161</ol>

3162</div>

3163</section>

3164<section id="_2275">

3165<h4 id="_2275_toc">

3166Chemotherapy for advanced disease</h4>

3167<p id="_2276" tabindex="-1">

3168Anthracyclines remain the first-line class of systemic therapy in managing most locally advanced and metastatic soft tissue sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.61">

316961</a>

3170] </p>

3171<p id="_2328" tabindex="-1">

3172Other regimens, approved for use as second-line therapy and beyond, include:</p>

3173<div class="pdq-content-list">

3174<ul id="_2329">

3175<li>

3176 Gemcitabine/docetaxel.[<a href="#cit/section_4.62">

317762</a>

3178] </li>

3179<li>

3180 Ifosfamide.</li>

3181<li>

3182 Trabectedin (liposarcoma and leiomyosarcoma).[<a href="#cit/section_4.63">

318363</a>

3184] </li>

3185<li>

3186 Eribulin (liposarcoma).[<a href="#cit/section_4.64">

318764</a>

3188] </li>

3189<li>

3190 Pazopanib.[<a href="#cit/section_4.65">

319165</a>

3192] </li>

3193<li>

3194 Dacarbazine.</li>

3195<li>

3196 Pegylated liposomal (encapsulated) doxorubicin.[<a href="#cit/section_4.66">

319766</a>

3198] </li>

3199</ul>

3200</div>

3201<p id="_2332" tabindex="-1">

3202Taxanes or taxane combinations are used for patients with angiosarcomas. </p>

3203<p id="_2278" tabindex="-1">

3204The clinical benefit of adding other drugs to the single-agent doxorubicin regimen is controversial. </p>

3205<p id="_2315" tabindex="-1">

3206In randomized studies, the combination of doxorubicin with ifosfamide has not demonstrated superiority over doxorubicin alone in terms of OS, but adding ifosfamide to doxorubicin may be considered in cases where reaching a better response to treatment, despite more toxicity, is the main treatment goal.[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

320752</a>

3208,<a href="#cit/section_4.67">

320967</a>

3210]</p>

3211</section>

3212</section>

3483 <h2>

3484Treatment of Stage I Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

3485

3486 <div id="_section_50" class="pdq-sections">

3487<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3488<h6>

3489In This Section</h6>

3490<ul>

3491<li>

3492<a href="#_1986_toc">

3494<li>

3495<a href="#_2063_toc">

3496Surgery</a>

3497</li>

3500Surgery with radiation therapy</a>

3501</li>

3502<li>

3507</a>

3515<section id="_1986">

3516<h3 id="_1986_toc">

3517Treatment Options for Stage I Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h3>

3518<p id="_1987" tabindex="-1">

3519Treatment options for <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1322">

3520stage I soft tissue sarcoma</a>

3521 include the following:</p>

3522<div class="pdq-content-list">

3523<ol id="_1988">

3524<li>

3525<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2063">

3526Surgery</a>

3527.</li>

3528<li>

3529<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1989">

3530Surgery with radiation therapy</a>

3531.</li>

3532<li>

3533<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2069">

3534High-dose radiation therapy</a>

3535.</li>

3536</ol>

3537</div>

3538<p id="_2323" tabindex="-1">

3539For more information on <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_278">

3540 surgery</a>

3541 and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_286">

3542radiation therapy</a>

3543, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_277">

3544Treatment Option Overview for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</a>

3545 section.</p>

3546<p id="_2303" tabindex="-1">

3547Because

3548of the low metastatic potential of these tumors, chemotherapy is usually not

3549administered to patients with stage I soft tissue sarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

35501</a>

3551,<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

35522</a>

3553]</p>

3554<section id="_2063">

3555<h4 id="_2063_toc">

3556Surgery</h4>

3557<p id="_2064" tabindex="-1">

3558Low-grade soft tissue sarcomas have little metastatic potential,

3559but they have a tendency to recur locally. The treatment of choice for patients with early-stage sarcomas (tumors &#8804;5 cm in diameters) is surgical excision with negative tissue margins (of 1 cm to 2 cm or larger) in all directions.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

35603</a>

3561-<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

356210</a>

3563]</p>

3564<p id="_2065" tabindex="-1">

3565The Mohs surgical technique may be considered as an alternative to wide

3566surgical excision for very rare, small, well-differentiated primary sarcomas of the skin when cosmetic results

3567are important, as margins can be assured with minimal

3568normal tissue removal.[<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

356911</a>

3570]</p>

3571</section>

3572<section id="_1989">

3573<h4 id="_1989_toc">

3574Surgery with radiation therapy</h4>

3575<p id="_2066" tabindex="-1">

3576Surgical excision with preoperative radiation therapy (preRT) or postoperative radiation

3577therapy (PORT) may be indicated. Radiation therapy decreases the risk of local recurrence but has not been shown to increase overall survival.[<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

357812</a>

3579-<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

358014</a>

3581]</p>

3582<p id="_2067" tabindex="-1">

3583For tumors of the retroperitoneum, trunk, and head and neck, the following are options:

3584</p>

3585<div class="pdq-content-list">

3586<ul id="_2068">

3587<li>

3588Surgical resection with the option of PORT if negative margins cannot be obtained. Wide margins are unusual in these sites, and radiation therapy is usually

3589advocated for trunk and head and neck primary tumor sites.[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

359015</a>

3591]</li>

3592<li>

3593PreRT followed by maximal surgical

3594resection. Radiation therapy may be used in sarcomas of the trunk and head and neck to maximize local

3595control when wide surgical margins cannot be obtained.[<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

359616</a>

3597]

3598

3599</li>

3600</ul>

3601</div>

3602</section>

3603<section id="_2069">

3604<h4 id="_2069_toc">

3605High-dose radiation therapy</h4>

3606<p id="_2070" tabindex="-1">

3607For unresectable tumors, higher doses of radiation therapy with curative intent may

3608be used.[<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

360917</a>

3610] Carefully executed high-dose radiation therapy using a shrinking-field technique may be beneficial in the following cases:[<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

361118</a>

3612]</p>

3613<div class="pdq-content-list">

3614<ul id="_2071">

3615<li>

3616Unresectable tumors.</li>

3617<li>

3618Resectable

3619tumors with inadequate margins and a high likelihood of residual disease.</li>

3620<li>

3621A wide resection

3622requires an amputation or the removal of a vital organ.</li>

3623</ul>

3624</div>

3625</section>

3626</section>

3627<section id="_TrialSearch_50_sid_5">

3628<h3 id="_TrialSearch_50_sid_5_toc">

3629Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3630<p id="_TrialSearch_50_22" tabindex="-1">

3631Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3632advanced clinical trial search</a>

3633 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3634General information</a>

3635 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3636</section>

3714Treatment of Stage II and Node-Negative Stage III Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

3715

3716 <div id="_section_61" class="pdq-sections">

3717<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3723Treatment Options for Stage II and Node-Negative Stage III Soft Tissue Sarcoma<ul>

3741</a>

3749<section id="_1998">

3750<h3 id="_1998_toc">

3751Treatment Options for Stage II and Node-Negative Stage III Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h3>

3752<p id="_1999" tabindex="-1">

3753Treatment options for <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1322">

3754stage II</a>

3755 and node-negative <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1322">

3756stage III soft tissue sarcoma</a>

3757 include the following:</p>

3758<div class="pdq-content-list">

3759<ol id="_2000">

3760<li>

3761<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2001">

3762Surgery</a>

3763.</li>

3764<li>

3765<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2003">

3766Surgery with radiation therapy</a>

3767.</li>

3768<li>

3769<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2075">

3770Radiation therapy and/or chemotherapy followed by surgery</a>

3771.</li>

3772<li>

3773<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2077">

3774High-dose radiation therapy</a>

3775.</li>

3776</ol>

3777</div>

3778<p id="_2324" tabindex="-1">

3779For more information on <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_278">

3780surgery</a>

3781, <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_286">

3782radiation therapy</a>

3783, and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_293">

3784chemotherapy</a>

3785, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_277">

3786Treatment Option Overview for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</a>

3787 section.</p>

3788<section id="_2001">

3789<h4 id="_2001_toc">

3790Surgery</h4>

3791<p id="_2002" tabindex="-1">

3792Complete surgical resection (removal of the entire gross tumor)

3793is the most important factor in preventing local recurrence and, in many

3794instances, requires resection of adjacent viscera. Surgical excision with negative tissue margins in all directions is generally restricted to low-grade tumors of the extremities (&#8804;5 cm in diameter) or superficial trunk tumors with microscopically negative surgical margins.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

37951</a>

3796-<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

37975</a>

3798]</p>

3799<p id="_2138" tabindex="-1">

3800Complete surgical resection of retroperitoneal sarcomas is often difficult because of their large size at detection and anatomical

3801location.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

38026</a>

3803,<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

38047</a>

3805] Local

3806recurrence is the most common cause of death in these patients. </p>

3807</section>

3808<section id="_2003">

3809<h4 id="_2003_toc">

3810 Surgery with radiation therapy</h4>

3811<p id="_2073" tabindex="-1">

3812High-grade localized soft tissue sarcomas have an increased potential for

3813local recurrence and metastasis. For sarcomas of the extremities, local control comparable

3814to that obtained with amputation may be achieved with limb-sparing surgery that

3815involves wide local excision in combination with preoperative radiation therapy (preRT) or postoperative

3816radiation therapy (PORT).

3817Radiation decreases the risk of local recurrence but has not been shown to increase overall survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

38188</a>

3819-<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

382011</a>

3821]</p>

3822<p id="_2074" tabindex="-1">

3823A retrospective review that compared surgery with or without preRT for retroperitoneal sarcomas suggested that the addition of preRT was associated with improved local recurrence-free survival, but not disease-free survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

382412</a>

3825]</p>

3826</section>

3827<section id="_2075">

3828<h4 id="_2075_toc">

3829Radiation therapy and/or chemotherapy followed by surgery</h4>

3830<p id="_2076" tabindex="-1">

3831In some situations, radiation therapy and/or chemotherapy

3832may be used before surgery to convert a marginally resectable tumor to one

3833that can be adequately resected with limb preservation. This treatment may be

3834followed by PORT.</p>

3835</section>

3836<section id="_2077">

3837<h4 id="_2077_toc">

3838High-dose radiation therapy</h4>

3839<p id="_2078" tabindex="-1">

3840For unresectable tumors, high-dose radiation therapy may be used, but

3841poor local control is likely to result.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

384213</a>

3843]</p>

3844</section>

3845</section>

3846<section id="_TrialSearch_61_sid_6">

3847<h3 id="_TrialSearch_61_sid_6_toc">

3848Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3849<p id="_TrialSearch_61_22" tabindex="-1">

3850Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3851advanced clinical trial search</a>

3852 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3853General information</a>

3854 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3855</section>

3920 <h2>

3921Treatment of Advanced Stage III (N1) Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

3922

3923 <div id="_section_313" class="pdq-sections">

3929<a href="#_2009_toc">

3930Treatment Options for Advanced Stage III (N1) Soft Tissue Sarcoma<ul>

3931<li>

3932<a href="#_2011_toc">

3933Surgery and lymphadenectomy</a>

3934</li>

3935<li>

3938</li>

3939</ul>

3940</a>

3941</li>
