3948<section id="_2009">

3949<h3 id="_2009_toc">

3950Treatment Options for Advanced Stage III (N1) Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h3>

3951<p id="_2015" tabindex="-1">

3952Treatment options for advanced <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1322">

3953stage III (N1) soft tissue sarcoma</a>

3954 include the following:</p>

3955<div class="pdq-content-list">

3956<ol id="_2010">

3957<li>

3958<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2011">

3959Surgery and lymphadenectomy</a>

3960.</li>

3961<li>

3962<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2013">

3963Surgery with neoadjuvant or adjuvant therapy</a>

3964.</li>

3965</ol>

3966</div>

3967<p id="_2325" tabindex="-1">

3968For more information on <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_278">

3969surgery</a>

3970, <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_286">

3971radiation therapy</a>

3972, and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_293">

3973chemotherapy</a>

3974, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_277">

3975Treatment Option Overview for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</a>

3976 section.</p>

3977<section id="_2011">

3978<h4 id="_2011_toc">

3979Surgery and lymphadenectomy</h4>

3980<p id="_2139" tabindex="-1">

3981Regional lymph node involvement by soft tissue sarcomas in adults is rare but may occur in some sarcoma types. The sarcoma types that more commonly spread to lymph nodes include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

39821</a>

3983]</p>

3984<div class="pdq-content-list">

3985<ul id="_2140">

3986<li>

3987High-grade rhabdomyosarcoma.</li>

3988<li>

3989Vascular sarcomas.</li>

3990<li>

3991Synovial sarcoma.</li>

3992<li>

3993High-grade fibrosarcoma.</li>

3994<li>

3995Clear cell sarcoma.</li>

3996<li>

3997Epithelioid sarcomas.</li>

3998</ul>

3999</div>

4000<p id="_2094" tabindex="-1">

4001Surgical resection and lymphadenectomy with or without postoperative radiation therapy may be indicated for patients with clinically positive lymph nodes.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

40021</a>

4003]</p>

4004</section>

4005<section id="_2013">

4006<h4 id="_2013_toc">

4007 Surgery with neoadjuvant or adjuvant therapy</h4>

4008<p id="_2079" tabindex="-1">

4009Neoadjuvant chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy or radiation therapy alone may be considered in selected cases where a limb-sparing surgery is advisable and/or there is a high probability of surgical resection with positive margins.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

40102</a>

4011-<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

40127</a>

4013] </p>

4014<p id="_2080" tabindex="-1">

4015Adjuvant chemotherapy may be considered but is not known to improve overall survival.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

40161</a>

4017,<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

40188</a>

4019-<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

402011</a>

4021] Clinical trials should be considered when available.</p>

4022</section>

4023</section>

4024<section id="_TrialSearch_313_sid_7">

4025<h3 id="_TrialSearch_313_sid_7_toc">

4026Current Clinical Trials</h3>

4027<p id="_TrialSearch_313_22" tabindex="-1">

4028Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4029advanced clinical trial search</a>

4030 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4031General information</a>

4032 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4122<section id="_2016">

4123<h3 id="_2016_toc">

4124Treatment Options for Stage IV Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h3>

4125<p id="_2017" tabindex="-1">

4126Treatment options for <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_1322">

4127stage IV soft tissue sarcoma</a>

4128 include the following:</p>

4129<div class="pdq-content-list">

4130<ol id="_2018">

4131<li>

4132<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2021">

4133Chemotherapy</a>

4134.</li>

4135<li>

4136<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2317">

4137Other agents after first-line chemotherapy</a>

4138.</li>

4139<li>

4140<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2320">

4141Surgery</a>

4142.</li>

4143</ol>

4144</div>

4145<p id="_2326" tabindex="-1">

4146For more information on <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_278">

4147surgery</a>

4148 and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_293">

4149chemotherapy</a>

4150, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_277">

4151Treatment Option Overview for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</a>

4152 section.</p>

4153<section id="_2021">

4154<h4 id="_2021_toc">

4155Chemotherapy</h4>

4156<p id="_2316" tabindex="-1">

4157Doxorubicin has been the standard systemic therapy in managing metastatic soft tissue sarcoma for several decades.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

41581</a>

4159-<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

41606</a>

4161] Other drugs that may have clinical activity as single agents or in combination with doxorubicin are ifosfamide, other anthracyclines (epirubicin, pegylated liposomal doxorubicin), gemcitabine, trabectedin, eribulin, pazopanib, dacarbazine, and taxanes.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

41624</a>

4163-<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

416410</a>

4165] Despite improved response rates with anthracycline combinations, toxicity is markedly higher without an improvement in overall survival (OS) for patients with soft tissue sarcoma, with the exception of some specific subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

416611</a>

4167] Thus, sequential use of single agents is the preferred strategy in most clinical settings.</p>

4168<p id="_2081" tabindex="-1">

4169 A variety of other regimens have been used, but none have increased OS when compared with doxorubicin alone.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

41701</a>

4171,<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

41722</a>

4173]</p>

4174<p id="_2082" tabindex="-1">

4175 There is some evidence that the addition of ifosfamide increases response rates, but not survival.</p>

4176<p id="_1579" tabindex="-1">

4177Evidence (doxorubicin and ifosfamide vs. doxorubicin alone):</p>

4178<div class="pdq-content-list">

4179<ol id="_1580">

4180<li>

4181A randomized study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00061984">

4182NCT00061984</a>

4183) assessed whether dose intensification of doxorubicin and ifosfamide improved the survival of patients with advanced soft tissue sarcoma compared with doxorubicin alone.[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

418411</a>

4185][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4186Level of evidence A1</a>

4187] In this study, 228 patients were randomly assigned to receive doxorubicin, and 227 patients were randomly assigned to receive doxorubicin and ifosfamide. The median follow-up was 56 months (interquartile range [IQR], 31&#8211;77 months) in the doxorubicin-only group and 59 months (IQR, 36&#8211;72 months) in the combination group.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4188<ol id="_2339" class="lower-alpha">

4189<li>

4190There was no significant difference in OS between groups: median OS was 12.8 months (95.5% confidence interval [CI], 10.5&#8211;14.3) in the doxorubicin-alone group versus 14.3 months (range, 12.5&#8211;16.5) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group (hazard ratio [HR], 0.83; 95.5% CI, 0.67&#8211;1.03; stratified log-rank test <em>

4191P</em>

4192 = .076). </li>

4193<li>

4194Median progression-free survival (PFS) was significantly higher for the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group (7.4 months; 95% CI, 6.6&#8211;8.3) than for the doxorubicin-alone group (4.6 months; range, 2.9&#8211;5.6 ) (HR, 0.74; 95% CI, 0.60&#8211;0.90; stratified log-rank test <em>

4195P</em>

4196 = .003). </li>

4197<li>

4198More patients in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group had an overall response (60 of 227 patients [26%]) than did patients in the doxorubicin-alone group (31 of 228 patients [14%]) (<em>

4199P</em>

4200 &lt; .0006). </li>

4201<li>

4202The most common grade 3 and 4 toxic effects were all more common with doxorubicin and ifosfamide (n = 224) than with doxorubicin alone (n = 223), as follows:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4203<ul id="_2092">

4204<li>

4205Leukopenia: 97 patients (43%) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group versus 40 patients (18%) in the doxorubicin-alone group.</li>

4206<li>

4207Neutropenia: 93 patients (42%) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group versus 83 patients (37%) in the doxorubicin-alone group.</li>

4208<li>

4209Febrile neutropenia: 103 patients (46%) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group versus 30 patients (13%) in the doxorubicin-alone group.</li>

4210<li>

4211Anemia: 78 patients (35%) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group versus 10 patients (5%) in the doxorubicin-alone group.</li>

4212<li>

4213Thrombocytopenia: 75 patients (33%) in the doxorubicin and ifosfamide group versus 1 patient (&lt;1%) in the doxorubicin-alone group.</li>

4214</ul>

4215</div>

4216</li>

4217</ol>

4218</div>

4219<p id="_368" tabindex="-1">

4220Based on these data, treatment intensification with doxorubicin and ifosfamide for palliation of advanced soft tissue sarcoma should not be considered outside of selected clinical settings.</p>

4221</li>

4222</ol>

4223</div>

4224<p id="_1362" tabindex="-1">

4225The combination of gemcitabine and docetaxel is used as second-line therapy in treating patients with soft tissue sarcoma. In selected cases, this combination may also be considered as first-line therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

42265</a>

4227,<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

422812</a>

4229] Toxicity is increased with the use of combination chemotherapy. However, no quality-of-life studies have compared the use of single-agent therapy with combination therapy.</p>

4230<p id="_2311" tabindex="-1">

4231Evidence (gemcitabine and docetaxel vs. gemcitabine alone):</p>

4232<div class="pdq-content-list">

4233<ol id="_2313">

4234<li>

4235A randomized phase II study of 122 patients with soft tissue sarcoma reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

42365</a>

4237][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4238Level of evidence B3</a>

4239]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4240<ul id="_2314">

4241<li>

4242The objective response rate was 16% in the gemcitabine and docetaxel group versus 8% in the gemcitabine-alone group.</li>

4243<li>

4244Median PFS was 6.2 months in the gemcitabine and docetaxel group versus 3.0 months in the gemcitabine-alone group.</li>

4245<li>

4246Median OS was 17.9 months in the gemcitabine and docetaxel group versus 11.5 months in the gemcitabine-alone group.</li>

4247</ul>

4248</div>

4249</li>

4250<li>

4251In a retrospective series of 133 patients, gemcitabine and docetaxel showed activity in patients with leiomyosarcoma as well as other histologies with an overall response rate of 18.4%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

425213</a>

4253]</li>

4254</ol>

4255</div>

4256<p id="_2175" tabindex="-1">

4257Evidence (gemcitabine and docetaxel vs. doxorubicin alone):</p>

4258<div class="pdq-content-list">

4259<ol id="_2141">

4260<li>

4261The GeDDis trial (<a href="http://www.isrctn.com/ISRCTN07742377" title="http://www.isrctn.com/ISRCTN07742377">

4262ISRCTN07742377</a>

4263) randomly assigned 257 previously untreated patients to receive either gemcitabine and docetaxel or doxorubicin alone.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

426412</a>

4265]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4266<ul id="_2142">

4267<li>

4268The PFS rate at 24 weeks (46% in both groups) and the primary end point (median PFS, 23.3 weeks vs. 23.7 weeks) were identical in both groups.</li>

4269<li>

4270This study may have been limited by the relatively low dose of gemcitabine used (675 mg/m<span class="sup">

42712</span>

4272 on days 1 and 8 instead of 900 mg/m<span class="sup">

42732</span>

4274 as in previous trials).</li>

4275</ul>

4276</div>

4277</li>

4278</ol>

4279</div>

4280<p id="_1364" tabindex="-1">

4281Although doxorubicin alone has traditionally been considered the standard when comparing new drugs or regimens in the context of phase III clinical trials, some sarcoma subtypes have shown higher sensitivity to specific agents. Some sarcoma subtypes are intrinsically resistant to traditional chemotherapy and referral for clinical trials or treatment with kinase inhibitors may be preferred. For examples of sarcoma subtypes with higher sensitivity to specific agents, see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2308">

4282Table 9</a>

4283.</p>

4284<table id="_2308" class="table-default expandable-container">

4348-fusion-associated sarcomas</td>

4349<td>

4350Larotrectinib [<a href="#cit/section_8.18">

435118</a>

4352]</td>

4353</tr>

4354<tr>

4355<td>

4356Epithelioid sarcoma</td>

4357<td>

4358Tazemetostat [<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

435919</a>

4360]</td>

4361</tr>

4362</tbody>

4363</table>

4364</section>

4365<section id="_2317">

4366<h4 id="_2317_toc">

4367Other agents after first-line chemotherapy</h4>

4368<p id="_2318" tabindex="-1">

4369After first-line chemotherapy, the following agents are standard options for patients with metastatic disease:</p>

4370<div class="pdq-content-list">

4371<ul id="_2319">

4372<li>

4373Gemcitabine combinations (gemcitabine plus docetaxel, gemcitabine plus vinorelbine, gemcitabine plus dacarbazine).</li>

4374<li>

4375Trabectedin for leiomyosarcoma and liposarcoma after anthracycline-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

43769</a>

4377] </li>

4378<li>

4379Eribulin for liposarcoma after anthracycline-based chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

438010</a>

4381]</li>

4382<li>

4383Pazopanib.[<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

43848</a>

4385]</li>

4386<li>

4387Ifosfamide or ifosfamide with etoposide.</li>

4388<li>

4389Dacarbazine.</li>

4390<li>

4391Pegylated liposomal doxorubicin.</li>

4392<li>

4393Temozolomide.</li>

4394<li>

4395Vinorelbine.</li>

4396<li>

4397Regorafenib.</li>

4398</ul>

4399</div>

4400</section>

4401<section id="_2320">

4402<h4 id="_2320_toc">

4403Surgery</h4>

4404<p id="_2321" tabindex="-1">

4405If the primary tumor is under control, resection of metastatic lung tumors may be associated with long-term disease-free survival in patients with optimal underlying disease biology, such as a limited number of metastases and slow tumor growth.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

440620</a>

4407-<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

440822</a>

4409] It is not clear whether favorable outcomes are attributable to the efficacy of surgery or to patient selection bias.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

441020</a>

4411-<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

441222</a>

4413] </p>

4414</section>

4421advanced clinical trial search</a>

4422 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4423General information</a>

4424 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4524 <section id="_90">

4525

4526 <h2>

4527Treatment of Recurrent Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h2>

4528

4529 <div id="_section_90" class="pdq-sections">

4530<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4531<h6>

4532In This Section</h6>

4533<ul>

4534<li>

4535<a href="#_2022_toc">

4536Treatment Options for Recurrent Soft Tissue Sarcoma<ul>

4537<li>

4538<a href="#_2023_toc">

4539Surgery with or without radiation therapy</a>

4540</li>

4541<li>

4542<a href="#_2025_toc">

4543Chemotherapy and targeted therapy</a>

4544</li>

4545<li>

4546<a href="#_2054_toc">

4547Immune checkpoint inhibitor therapy</a>

4548</li>

4549</ul>

4550</a>

4551</li>

4552<li>

4553<a href="#_TrialSearch_90_sid_9_toc">

4554Current Clinical Trials</a>

4555</li>

4556</ul>

4557</nav>

4558<p id="_92" tabindex="-1">

4559Treatment of patients with recurrent soft tissue sarcoma depends on the

4560clinical presentation of the disease and previous treatment.</p>

4561<section id="_2022">

4562<h3 id="_2022_toc">

4563Treatment Options for Recurrent Soft Tissue Sarcoma</h3>

4564<p id="_1586" tabindex="-1">

4565Treatment options for recurrent soft tissue sarcoma include the following:</p>

4566<div class="pdq-content-list">

4567<ol id="_1587">

4568<li>

4569<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2023">

4570Surgery with or without radiation therapy</a>

4571.</li>

4572<li>

4573<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2025">

4574Chemotherapy and targeted therapy</a>

4575.</li>

4576<li>

4577<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2054">

4578Immune checkpoint inhibitor therapy</a>

4579.</li>

4580</ol>

4581</div>

4582<p id="_2327" tabindex="-1">

4583For more information on <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_278">

4584surgery</a>

4585, <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_286">

4586radiation therapy</a>

4587, and <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_293">

4588chemotherapy</a>

4589, see the <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_277">

4590Treatment Option Overview for Soft Tissue Sarcoma</a>

4591 section.</p>

4592<section id="_2023">

4593<h4 id="_2023_toc">

4594Surgery with or without radiation therapy</h4>

4595<p id="_2030" tabindex="-1">

4596Patients who develop a local recurrence can

4597often be treated with local therapy, such as surgical excision plus radiation

4598therapy (after previous minimal therapy) or amputation (after previous aggressive

4599treatment).[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

46001</a>

4601-<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

46027</a>

4603] Resection of limited pulmonary metastases may be associated with favorable disease-free survival.[<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

46048</a>

4605-<a href="#cit/section_9.10">

460610</a>

4607][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4608Level of evidence C3</a>

4609] However, the contribution of selection factors, such as low tumor burden, slow tumor growth, and long disease-free interval, to these favorable outcomes is not known.</p>

4610</section>

4611<section id="_2025">

4612<h4 id="_2025_toc">

4613Chemotherapy and targeted therapy</h4>

4614<p id="_2036" tabindex="-1">

4615Single-agent chemotherapy may be used with other single agents for disease recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

461611</a>

4617-<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

461816</a>

4619] Agents such as ifosfamide or gemcitabine may be used sequentially at the time of recurrence or progression.[<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

462013</a>

4621-<a href="#cit/section_9.15">

462215</a>

4623,<a href="#cit/section_9.17">

462417</a>

4625][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4626Level of evidence C3</a>

4627] However, none of these agents has been shown to increase overall survival (OS) in this setting; therefore, clinical trials are an appropriate option.</p>

4628<p id="_2085" tabindex="-1">

4629Since 2012, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) has approved three drugs for the treatment of soft tissue sarcomas after failure of a first-line chemotherapy regimen:</p>

4630<div class="pdq-content-list">

4631<ul id="_2086">

4632<li>

4633<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2144">

4634Eribulin</a>

4635 for liposarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.18">

463618</a>

4637]</li>

4638<li>

4639<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2149">

4640Trabectedin</a>

4641 for leiomyosarcoma and liposarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

464219</a>

4643]</li>

4644<li>

4645<a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq#_2155">

4646Pazopanib</a>

4647 for all soft tissue sarcomas except adipocytic subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

464820</a>

4649]</li>

4650</ul>

4651</div>

4652<section id="_2144">

4653<h5 id="_2144_toc">

4654Eribulin</h5>

4655<p id="_2145" tabindex="-1">

4656Eribulin is a microtubule inhibitor that the FDA approved in 2016 to treat patients with unresectable or metastatic liposarcoma, who previously received anthracycline-containing chemotherapy.</p>

4657<p id="_2146" tabindex="-1">

4658Evidence (eribulin):</p>

4659<div class="pdq-content-list">

4660<ol id="_2147">

4661<li>

4662The approval of eribulin was based on a phase III, multicenter, randomized study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01327885">

4663NCT01327885</a>

4664) of 452 patients with advanced leiomyosarcoma or adipocytic sarcoma who received eribulin (1.4 mg/m<span class="sup">

46652</span>

4666 intravenously [IV] on days 1 and 8 every 3 weeks) versus dacarbazine (850&#8211;1,200 mg/m<span class="sup">

46672</span>

4668 IV on day 1 every 3 weeks).[<a href="#cit/section_9.18">

466918</a>

4670]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4671<ul id="_2148">

4672<li>

4673Although the median OS was 13.5 months for patients who received eribulin versus 11.5 months for patients who received dacarbazine (hazard ratio [HR]<sub>

4674death</sub>

4675, 0.77; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.62&#8211;0.95), a preplanned subset analysis revealed a median survival of 15.6 months for eribulin versus 8.4 months for dacarbazine in patients with liposarcoma.</li>

4676<li>

4677Median progression-free survival (PFS) was the same in both groups (2.6 months).</li>

4678</ul>

4679</div>

4680</li>

4681</ol>

4682</div>

4683</section>

4684<section id="_2149">

4685<h5 id="_2149_toc">

4686Trabectedin</h5>

4687<p id="_2150" tabindex="-1">

4688Trabectedin is an FDA-approved option for second-line treatment of patients with advanced liposarcoma and leiomyosarcoma. </p>

4689<p id="_2151" tabindex="-1">

4690Evidence (trabectedin):</p>

4691<div class="pdq-content-list">

4692<ol id="_2152">

4693<li>

4694The approval of trabectedin was based on a phase III randomized study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01343277">

4695NCT01343277</a>

4696) of 518 patients who received trabectedin (1.5 mg/m<span class="sup">

46972</span>

4698 over 24 hours on day 1 every 21 days) or dacarbazine (1,000 mg/m<span class="sup">

46992</span>

4700 on day 1 every 21 days).[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

470119</a>

4702]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4703<ul id="_2153">

4704<li>

4705Treatment with trabectedin significantly improved median PFS (4.2 months for patients who received trabectedin vs. 1.5 months for patients who received dacarbazine). </li>

4706<li>

4707OS, the primary end point, was not statistically different (12.4 months for patients who received trabectedin vs. 12.9 months for patients who received dacarbazine). </li>

4708<li>

4709Response rates were low in both arms (10% for patients who received trabectedin vs. 7% for patients who received dacarbazine), but clinical benefit (that included both response rates and stable disease) was higher for the trabectedin group (34%) than for the dacarbazine group (19%). </li>

4710<li>

4711The most common grade 3 and 4 adverse events in the trabectedin group were myelosuppression and transient elevation of liver function test results.</li>

4712</ul>

4713</div>

4714</li>

4715</ol>

4716</div>

4717<p id="_2154" tabindex="-1">

4718Phase II studies have shown a particularly high response rate to trabectedin in patients with myxoid/round cell liposarcoma, with overall response rates up to 51%, and a 6-month PFS rate of 88%.[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

471921</a>

4720]</p>

4721</section>

4722<section id="_2155">

4723<h5 id="_2155_toc">

4724Pazopanib</h5>

4725<p id="_2156" tabindex="-1">

4726Pazopanib is a multitargeted, oral, small molecule inhibitor of several tyrosine kinases, including vascular endothelial growth factor receptor-1, -2, and -3; platelet-derived growth factor receptor alpha and beta; and fibroblast growth factor receptor-1, and -3.</p>

4727<p id="_2157" tabindex="-1">

4728Evidence (pazopanib):</p>

4729<div class="pdq-content-list">

4730<ol id="_2158">

4731<li>

4732The phase III, randomized, double-blind <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00753688">

4733PALETTE</a>

4734 study (NCT00753688) from the European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer compared pazopanib (800 mg daily) with placebo in 369 patients with different soft tissue sarcoma subtypes, excluding adipocytic sarcomas and gastrointestinal stromal tumors. Patients were enrolled after disease progression on a first-line anthracycline-based regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

473520</a>

4736]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4737<ul id="_2159">

4738<li>

4739The median PFS was 4.6 months for patients who received pazopanib versus 1.6 months for patients who received placebo. </li>

4740<li>

4741The OS difference was not statistically significant. OS was 12.5 months for patients who received pazopanib versus 10.7 months for patients who received placebo (HR, 0.86; 95% CI, 0.67&#8211;1.1). </li>

4742<li>

4743Overall response rate was 6% for patients who received pazopanib versus 0% for patients who received placebo.</li>

4744<li>

4745The stability of disease rate was 67% for patients who received pazopanib versus 38% for patients who received placebo.</li>

4746<li>

4747The most common grade 3 or 4 toxicities in the pazopanib arm were fatigue, hypertension, diarrhea, anorexia, and transient elevation of liver function test results. </li>

4748</ul>

4749</div>

4750</li>

4751</ol>

4752</div>

4753<p id="_2160" tabindex="-1">

4754On the basis of the above data, pazopanib was approved by the FDA in 2012 for the treatment of patients with soft tissue sarcomas (except the adipocytic subtypes) who have received previous chemotherapy.</p>

4755</section>

4756</section>

4757<section id="_2054">

4758<h4 id="_2054_toc">

4759Immune checkpoint inhibitor therapy</h4>

4760<p id="_2055" tabindex="-1">

4761Two trials have explored the immune checkpoint inhibitors pembrolizumab, nivolumab, and ipilimumab. Although some activity has been shown in selected soft tissue sarcoma subtypes, the factors that may predict activity to treatment with immune checkpoint inhibitors remain unknown, and their use cannot be routinely recommended.</p>

4762<p id="_2056" tabindex="-1">

4763Evidence (immune checkpoint inhibitors):</p>

4764<div class="pdq-content-list">

4765<ol id="_2057">

4766<li>

4767The phase II Sarcoma Alliance for Research through Collaboration trial (<a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT02301039" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT02301039">

4768SARC028</a>

4769 [NCT02301039]) studied treatment with pembrolizumab alone in patients with four soft tissue sarcoma subtypes: undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma, synovial sarcoma, leiomyosarcoma, and poorly differentiated or dedifferentiated liposarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.22">

477022</a>

4771] Eighty patients were evaluable for response, with half of the patients in the soft tissue sarcoma group and half in the bone sarcoma cohort. <p id="_2306" tabindex="-1">

4772Responses were noted in the following subtypes:</p>

4773<div class="pdq-content-list">

4774<ul id="_2058">

4775<li>

4776Four of 10 patients with undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma.</li>

4777<li>

4778Two of 10 patients with poorly differentiated or dedifferentiated liposarcoma.</li>

4779<li>

4780One of 10 patients with synovial sarcoma.</li>

4781<li>

4782Zero of 10 patients with leiomyosarcoma.</li>

4783<li>

4784The overall response rate was 18%.</li>

4785</ul>

4786</div>

4787</li>

4788<li>

4789The phase II <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02500797">

4790Alliance A091401</a>

4791 study (NCT02500797) randomly assigned patients to receive nivolumab (n = 43) versus nivolumab and ipilimumab (n = 42) for the treatment of different soft tissue sarcomas.[<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

479223</a>

4793] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4794<ul id="_2059">

4795<li>

4796Two patients in the nivolumab group had an objective response (5%; one patient with alveolar soft-part sarcoma and one patient with leiomyosarcoma). Six patients in the combination group had an objective response (16%; two patients with leiomyosarcoma, one patient with myxofibrosarcoma, two patients with undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma, and one patient with angiosarcoma). </li>

4797</ul>

4798</div>

4799</li>

4800</ol>

4801</div>

4802</section>

4803</section>

4804<section id="_TrialSearch_90_sid_9">

4805<h3 id="_TrialSearch_90_sid_9_toc">

4806Current Clinical Trials</h3>

4807<p id="_TrialSearch_90_22" tabindex="-1">

4808Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4809advanced clinical trial search</a>

4810 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4811General information</a>

4812 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4949 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

4950

4951 <h2>

4952About This PDQ Summary</h2>

4953

4954 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

4955<section id="_AboutThis_2">

4956<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

4957Purpose of This Summary</h3>

4958<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

4959This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of soft tissue sarcoma. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

4960</section>

4961<section id="_AboutThis_4">

4962<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

4963Reviewers and Updates</h3>

4964<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

4965This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

4966PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

4967, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

4968<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

4969 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

4970<div class="pdq-content-list">

4971<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

4972<li>

4973be discussed at a meeting,</li>

4974<li>

4975be cited with text, or</li>

4976<li>

4977replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

4978</ul>

4979</div>

4980<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

4981Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

4982<p id="" tabindex="-1">

4983The lead reviewers for Soft Tissue Sarcoma Treatment are:</p>

4984<div class="pdq-content-list">

4985<ul id="">

4986<li>

4987Russell S. Berman, MD (New York University School of Medicine)</li>

4988<li>

4989Minh Tam Truong, MD (Boston University Medical Center)</li>

4990<li>

4991Vinayak Venkataraman, MD</li>

4992</ul>

4993</div>

4994<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

4995Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

4996Email Us</a>

4997. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

4998</section>

4999<section id="_AboutThis_10">

5000<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

5001Levels of Evidence</h3>

5002<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

5003Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

5004formal evidence ranking system</a>

5005 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

5006</section>

5007<section id="_AboutThis_12">

5008<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

5009Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

5010<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

5011PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

5012<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

5013The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

5014<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

5015PDQ&#174; Adult Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Soft Tissue Sarcoma Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq">

5016https://www.cancer.gov/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/adult-soft-tissue-treatment-pdq</a>

5017. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389481]</p>

5018<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

5019Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

5020Visuals Online</a>

5021, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

5024</section>

5025<section id="_AboutThis_17">

5026<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

5027Disclaimer</h3>

5028<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

5029Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

5030Managing Cancer Care</a>

5031 page.</p>
